<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Swedish botanist, physician, and zoologist (1707–1778)</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus_the_Younger" title="Carl Linnaeus the Younger">Carl Linnaeus the Younger</a> or <a href="/wiki/Karl_Linnas" title="Karl Linnas">Karl Linnas</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"L.", "Linn.", and "Linnaeus" redirect here. <p><b>Carl Linnaeus</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[2]</a></sup> 23 May<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-birthdate_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-birthdate-1">[note 1]</a></sup> 1707 – 10 January 1778), also known after his <a href="/wiki/Nobility#Ennoblement" title="Nobility">ennoblement</a> in 1761 as <b>Carl von Linné</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt171_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt171-4">[3]</a></sup> (<small>Swedish pronunciation: </small><span class="IPA" lang="sv-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Swedish" title="Help:IPA/Swedish">[ˈkɑːɭ fɔn lɪˈneː]</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sv-Carl_von_Linn%C3%A9.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/68/Sv-Carl_von_Linn%C3%A9.ogg" title="Sv-Carl von Linné.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span>), was a Swedish <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Botanist" title="Botanist">botanist</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zoologist" title="Zoologist">zoologist</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taxonomist" title="Taxonomist">taxonomist</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physician</a> who formalised <a href="/wiki/Binomial_nomenclature" title="Binomial nomenclature">binomial nomenclature</a>, the modern system of naming organisms. He is known as the "father of modern <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomy</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-calisher_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-calisher-5">[4]</a></sup> Many of his writings were in Latin; his name is rendered in Latin as <b><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Carolus Linnæus</i></span></b> and, after his 1761 ennoblement, as <b><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Carolus a Linné</i></span></b>.
</p><p>Linnaeus was born in <a href="/wiki/R%C3%A5shult" title="Råshult">Råshult</a>, the countryside of <a href="/wiki/Sm%C3%A5land" title="Småland">Småland</a>, in southern Sweden. He received most of his higher education at <a href="/wiki/Uppsala_University" title="Uppsala University">Uppsala University</a> and began giving lectures in botany there in 1730. He lived abroad between 1735 and 1738, where he studied and also published the first edition of his <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Systema_Naturae" title="Systema Naturae">Systema Naturae</a></i></span></i> in the Netherlands. He then returned to Sweden where he became professor of medicine and botany at Uppsala. In the 1740s, he was sent on several journeys through Sweden to find and classify plants and animals. In the 1750s and 1760s, he continued to collect and classify animals, plants, and minerals, while publishing several volumes. He was one of the most acclaimed scientists in Europe at the time of his death.
</p><p>Philosopher <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau" title="Jean-Jacques Rousseau">Jean-Jacques Rousseau</a> sent him the message: "Tell him I know no greater man on earth."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-6">[5]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Johann Wolfgang von Goethe</a> wrote: "With the exception of Shakespeare and <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza" title="Baruch Spinoza">Spinoza</a>, I know no one among the no longer living who has influenced me more strongly."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-6">[5]</a></sup> Swedish author <a href="/wiki/August_Strindberg" title="August Strindberg">August Strindberg</a> wrote: "Linnaeus was in reality a poet who happened to become a naturalist."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-deceased_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-deceased-7">[6]</a></sup> Linnaeus has been called <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Princeps botanicorum</i></span></i> (Prince of Botanists) and "The <a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)">Pliny</a> of the North".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Broberg7_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Broberg7-8">[7]</a></sup> He is also considered one of the founders of modern <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">ecology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>In botany and zoology, the abbreviation <b>L.</b> is used to indicate Linnaeus as the authority for a species' name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup> In older publications, the abbreviation "Linn." is found. Linnaeus's remains constitute the <a href="/wiki/Type_(biology)#Lectotype" title="Type (biology)">type specimen</a> for the species <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i> following the <a href="/wiki/International_Code_of_Zoological_Nomenclature" title="International Code of Zoological Nomenclature">International Code of Zoological Nomenclature</a>, since the sole specimen that he is known to have examined was himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-holotype_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-holotype-11">[note 2]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood">Childhood</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Linn%C3%A9_family" title="Linné family">Linné family</a></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LA2-Rashult-2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/LA2-Rashult-2.jpg/220px-LA2-Rashult-2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/LA2-Rashult-2.jpg/330px-LA2-Rashult-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/LA2-Rashult-2.jpg/440px-LA2-Rashult-2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LA2-Rashult-2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Birthplace at <a href="/wiki/R%C3%A5shult" title="Råshult">Råshult</a></div></div></div>
<p>Linnaeus was born in the village of <a href="/wiki/R%C3%A5shult" title="Råshult">Råshult</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sm%C3%A5land" title="Småland">Småland</a>, Sweden, on 23 May 1707. He was the first child of Nicolaus (Nils) Ingemarsson (who later adopted the family name Linnaeus) and Christina Brodersonia. His siblings were Anna Maria Linnæa, Sofia Juliana Linnæa, Samuel Linnæus (who would eventually succeed their father as rector of Stenbrohult and write a manual on <a href="/wiki/Beekeeping" title="Beekeeping">beekeeping</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt12_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt12-12">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stöver8_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stöver8-13">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Broberg10_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Broberg10-14">[12]</a></sup> and Emerentia Linnæa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[13]</a></sup> His father taught him Latin as a small child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of a long line of peasants and priests, Nils was an amateur <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Botanist" title="Botanist">botanist</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lutheran" title="Lutheran">Lutheran</a> <a href="/wiki/Minister_(Christianity)" title="Minister (Christianity)">minister</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Curate" title="Curate">curate</a> of the small village of Stenbrohult in Småland. Christina was the daughter of the <a href="/wiki/Rector_(ecclesiastical)" title="Rector (ecclesiastical)">rector</a> of Stenbrohult, Samuel Brodersonius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fries2011_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fries2011-17">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 376">: 376 </span></sup>
</p><p>A year after Linnaeus's birth, his grandfather Samuel Brodersonius died, and his father Nils became the rector of Stenbrohult. The family moved into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rectory" title="Rectory">rectory</a> from the curate's house.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt13_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt13-18">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nationalgeographic2007_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-nationalgeographic2007-19">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Even in his early years, Linnaeus seemed to have a liking for plants, flowers in particular. Whenever he was upset, he was given a flower, which immediately calmed him. Nils spent much time in his garden and often showed flowers to Linnaeus and told him their names. Soon Linnaeus was given his own patch of earth where he could grow plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt15_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt15-20">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Carl's father was the first in his ancestry to adopt a permanent surname. Before that, ancestors had used the <a href="/wiki/Patronymic" title="Patronymic">patronymic</a> naming system of Scandinavian countries: his father was named Ingemarsson after his father Ingemar Bengtsson. When Nils was admitted to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Lund" title="University of Lund">University of Lund</a>, he had to take on a family name. He adopted the Latinate name Linnæus after a giant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linden_tree" title="Linden tree">linden tree</a> (or lime tree), <i><span title="Swedish-language text"><i lang="sv">lind</i></span></i> in Swedish, that grew on the family homestead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt12_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt12-12">[10]</a></sup> This name was spelled with the <a href="/wiki/%C3%86" title="Æ">æ</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Typographic_ligature" title="Typographic ligature">ligature</a>. When Carl was born, he was named Carl Linnæus, with his father's family name. The son also always spelled it with the æ ligature, both in handwritten documents and in publications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt13_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt13-18">[16]</a></sup> Carl's patronymic would have been Nilsson, as in Carl Nilsson Linnæus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_education">Early education</span></h3>
<p>Linnaeus's father began teaching him basic Latin, religion, and geography at an early age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[20]</a></sup> When Linnaeus was seven, Nils decided to hire a tutor for him. The parents picked Johan Telander, a son of a local <a href="/wiki/Yeoman" title="Yeoman">yeoman</a>. Linnaeus did not like him, writing in his autobiography that Telander "was better calculated to extinguish a child's talents than develop them".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt15-16_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt15-16-23">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Two years after his tutoring had begun, he was sent to the Lower <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grammar_School" title="Grammar School">Grammar School</a> at <a href="/wiki/V%C3%A4xj%C3%B6" title="Växjö">Växjö</a> in 1717.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stöver5_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stöver5-24">[22]</a></sup> Linnaeus rarely studied, often going to the countryside to look for plants. At some point, his father went to visit him and, after hearing critical assessements by his preceptors, he decided to put the youth as an apprentice to some honest cobbler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup> He reached the last year of the Lower School when he was fifteen, which was taught by the headmaster, Daniel Lannerus, who was interested in botany. Lannerus noticed Linnaeus's interest in botany and gave him the run of his garden.
</p><p>He also introduced him to Johan Rothman, the state doctor of Småland and a teacher at <a href="/wiki/Katedralskolan,_V%C3%A4xj%C3%B6" title="Katedralskolan, Växjö">Katedralskolan</a> (a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gymnasieskola" title="Gymnasieskola">gymnasium</a>) in Växjö. Also a botanist, Rothman broadened Linnaeus's interest in botany and helped him develop an interest in medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt16_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt16-26">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stöver5-6_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stöver5-6-27">[25]</a></sup> By the age of 17, Linnaeus had become well acquainted with the existing botanical literature. He remarks in his journal that he "read day and night, knowing like the back of my hand, Arvidh Månsson's Rydaholm Book of Herbs, Tillandz's Flora Åboensis, <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Palmberg" title="Johannes Palmberg">Palmberg's</a> Serta Florea Suecana,  Bromelii's Chloros Gothica and Rudbeckii's Hortus Upsaliensis".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Linnaeus entered the Växjö Katedralskola in 1724, where he studied mainly <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hebrew" title="Hebrew">Hebrew</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, a curriculum designed for boys preparing for the priesthood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stöver6_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stöver6-29">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt16-17_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt16-17-30">[28]</a></sup> In the last year at the gymnasium, Linnaeus's father visited to ask the professors how his son's studies were progressing; to his dismay, most said that the boy would never become a scholar. Rothman believed otherwise, suggesting Linnaeus could have a future in medicine. The doctor offered to have Linnaeus live with his family in Växjö and to teach him <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiology</a> and botany. Nils accepted this offer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt17-18_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt17-18-31">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stöver8-11_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stöver8-11-32">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="University_studies">University studies</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lund">Lund</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carl_von_Linn%C3%A9_i_Lund.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="768" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Carl_von_Linn%C3%A9_i_Lund.jpg/170px-Carl_von_Linn%C3%A9_i_Lund.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Carl_von_Linn%C3%A9_i_Lund.jpg/255px-Carl_von_Linn%C3%A9_i_Lund.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Carl_von_Linn%C3%A9_i_Lund.jpg/340px-Carl_von_Linn%C3%A9_i_Lund.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carl_von_Linn%C3%A9_i_Lund.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue as a university student in <a href="/wiki/Lund" title="Lund">Lund</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Ansgar_Almquist" title="Ansgar Almquist">Ansgar Almquist</a></div></div></div>
<p>Rothman showed Linnaeus that botany was a serious subject. He taught Linnaeus to classify plants according to <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Pitton_de_Tournefort" title="Joseph Pitton de Tournefort">Tournefort's</a> system. Linnaeus was also taught about the sexual reproduction of plants, according to <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A9bastien_Vaillant" title="Sébastien Vaillant">Sébastien Vaillant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt17-18_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt17-18-31">[29]</a></sup> In 1727, Linnaeus, age 21, enrolled in <a href="/wiki/Lund_University" title="Lund University">Lund University</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sk%C3%A5ne" title="Skåne">Skåne</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt18_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt18-33">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stöver13_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stöver13-34">[32]</a></sup> He was registered as <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Carolus Linnæus</i></span></i>, the Latin form of his full name, which he also used later for his Latin publications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt171_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt171-4">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Professor <a href="/wiki/Kilian_Stob%C3%A6us" title="Kilian Stobæus">Kilian Stobæus</a>, natural scientist, physician and historian, offered Linnaeus tutoring and lodging, as well as the use of his library, which included many books about botany. He also gave the student free admission to his lectures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt21-22_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt21-22-35">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stöver15_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stöver15-36">[34]</a></sup> In his spare time, Linnaeus explored the flora of Skåne, together with students sharing the same interests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stöver14-15_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stöver14-15-37">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Uppsala">Uppsala</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Linn%C3%A9-Praeludia_Sponsaliorum_Plantarum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1676" data-file-width="2148" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Linn%C3%A9-Praeludia_Sponsaliorum_Plantarum.jpg/220px-Linn%C3%A9-Praeludia_Sponsaliorum_Plantarum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Linn%C3%A9-Praeludia_Sponsaliorum_Plantarum.jpg/330px-Linn%C3%A9-Praeludia_Sponsaliorum_Plantarum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Linn%C3%A9-Praeludia_Sponsaliorum_Plantarum.jpg/440px-Linn%C3%A9-Praeludia_Sponsaliorum_Plantarum.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Linn%C3%A9-Praeludia_Sponsaliorum_Plantarum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pollination" title="Pollination">Pollination</a> depicted in <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Praeludia Sponsaliorum Plantarum</i></span></i> (1729)</div></div></div>
<p>In August 1728, Linnaeus decided to attend <a href="/wiki/Uppsala_University" title="Uppsala University">Uppsala University</a> on the advice of Rothman, who believed it would be a better choice if Linnaeus wanted to study both medicine and botany. Rothman based this recommendation on the two professors who taught at the medical faculty at Uppsala: <a href="/wiki/Olof_Rudbeck_the_Younger" title="Olof Rudbeck the Younger">Olof Rudbeck the Younger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lars_Roberg" title="Lars Roberg">Lars Roberg</a>. Although Rudbeck and Roberg had undoubtedly been good professors, by then they were older and not so interested in teaching. Rudbeck no longer gave public lectures, and had others stand in for him. The botany, zoology, pharmacology and anatomy lectures were not in their best state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt23-25_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt23-25-38">[36]</a></sup> In Uppsala, Linnaeus met a new benefactor, <a href="/wiki/Olof_Celsius" title="Olof Celsius">Olof Celsius</a>, who was a professor of theology and an amateur botanist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt31-32_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt31-32-39">[37]</a></sup> He received Linnaeus into his home and allowed him use of his library, which was one of the richest botanical libraries in Sweden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stöver19-20_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stöver19-20-40">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1729, Linnaeus wrote a thesis, <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Praeludia Sponsaliorum Plantarum</i></span></i> on <a href="/wiki/Plant_reproductive_morphology" title="Plant reproductive morphology">plant sexual reproduction</a>. This attracted the attention of Rudbeck; in May 1730, he selected Linnaeus to give lectures at the University although the young man was only a second-year student. His lectures were popular, and Linnaeus often addressed an audience of 300 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt32-34_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt32-34-41">[39]</a></sup> In June, Linnaeus moved from Celsius's house to Rudbeck's to become the tutor of the three youngest of his 24 children. His friendship with Celsius did not wane and they continued their botanical expeditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt34-37_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt34-37-42">[40]</a></sup> Over that winter, Linnaeus began to doubt Tournefort's system of classification and decided to create one of his own. His plan was to divide the plants by the number of <a href="/wiki/Stamen" title="Stamen">stamens</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pistil" title="Pistil">pistils</a>. He began writing several books, which would later result in, for example, <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Genera_Plantarum" title="Genera Plantarum">Genera Plantarum</a></i></span></i> and <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Critica_Botanica" title="Critica Botanica">Critica Botanica</a></i></span></i>. He also produced a book on the plants grown in the <a href="/wiki/Linnaean_Garden" title="Linnaean Garden">Uppsala Botanical Garden</a>, <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Adonis Uplandicus</i></span></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt2001-36-37_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt2001-36-37-43">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rudbeck's former assistant, <a href="/wiki/Nils_Ros%C3%A9n_von_Rosenstein" title="Nils Rosén von Rosenstein">Nils Rosén</a>, returned to the University in March 1731 with a degree in medicine. Rosén started giving anatomy lectures and tried to take over Linnaeus's botany lectures, but Rudbeck prevented that. Until December, Rosén gave Linnaeus private tutoring in medicine. In December, Linnaeus had a "disagreement" with Rudbeck's wife and had to move out of his mentor's house; his relationship with Rudbeck did not appear to suffer. That Christmas, Linnaeus returned home to Stenbrohult to visit his parents for the first time in about three years. His mother had disapproved of his failing to become a priest, but she was pleased to learn he was teaching at the University.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt2001-36-37_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt2001-36-37-43">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson40_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson40-44">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Expedition_to_Lapland">Expedition to Lapland</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Expedition_to_Lapland" title="Expedition to Lapland">Expedition to Lapland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flora_Lapponica" title="Flora Lapponica">Flora Lapponica</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carolus_Linnaeus_by_Hendrik_Hollander_1853-2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="914" data-file-width="747" decoding="async" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Carolus_Linnaeus_by_Hendrik_Hollander_1853-2.jpg/220px-Carolus_Linnaeus_by_Hendrik_Hollander_1853-2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Carolus_Linnaeus_by_Hendrik_Hollander_1853-2.jpg/330px-Carolus_Linnaeus_by_Hendrik_Hollander_1853-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Carolus_Linnaeus_by_Hendrik_Hollander_1853-2.jpg/440px-Carolus_Linnaeus_by_Hendrik_Hollander_1853-2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carolus_Linnaeus_by_Hendrik_Hollander_1853-2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Carl Linnaeus in Laponian costume (1737)</div></div></div>
<p>During a visit with his parents, Linnaeus told them about his plan to travel to <a href="/wiki/Lapland_(Sweden)" title="Lapland (Sweden)">Lapland</a>; Rudbeck had made the journey in 1695, but the detailed results of his exploration were lost in a fire seven years afterwards. Linnaeus's hope was to find new plants, animals and possibly valuable minerals. He was also curious about the customs of the native <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sami_people" title="Sami people">Sami people</a>, reindeer-herding nomads who wandered Scandinavia's vast tundras. In April 1732, Linnaeus was awarded a grant from the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_of_Sciences_in_Uppsala" title="Royal Society of Sciences in Uppsala">Royal Society of Sciences in Uppsala</a> for his journey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson42-43_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson42-43-45">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt2001-38_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt2001-38-46">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Naturalis_Biodiversity_Center_-_Martin_Hoffman_-_Carl_von_Linn%C3%A9_(Linnaeus)_in_his_Lapland_costume_-_painting.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5846" data-file-width="3327" decoding="async" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Naturalis_Biodiversity_Center_-_Martin_Hoffman_-_Carl_von_Linn%C3%A9_%28Linnaeus%29_in_his_Lapland_costume_-_painting.jpg/170px-Naturalis_Biodiversity_Center_-_Martin_Hoffman_-_Carl_von_Linn%C3%A9_%28Linnaeus%29_in_his_Lapland_costume_-_painting.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Naturalis_Biodiversity_Center_-_Martin_Hoffman_-_Carl_von_Linn%C3%A9_%28Linnaeus%29_in_his_Lapland_costume_-_painting.jpg/255px-Naturalis_Biodiversity_Center_-_Martin_Hoffman_-_Carl_von_Linn%C3%A9_%28Linnaeus%29_in_his_Lapland_costume_-_painting.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Naturalis_Biodiversity_Center_-_Martin_Hoffman_-_Carl_von_Linn%C3%A9_%28Linnaeus%29_in_his_Lapland_costume_-_painting.jpg/340px-Naturalis_Biodiversity_Center_-_Martin_Hoffman_-_Carl_von_Linn%C3%A9_%28Linnaeus%29_in_his_Lapland_costume_-_painting.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Naturalis_Biodiversity_Center_-_Martin_Hoffman_-_Carl_von_Linn%C3%A9_(Linnaeus)_in_his_Lapland_costume_-_painting.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wearing the traditional dress of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sami_people" title="Sami people">Sami people</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lapland_(region)" title="Lapland (region)">Lapland</a>, holding the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twinflower" title="Twinflower">twinflower</a>, later known as <i>Linnaea borealis</i>, that became his personal emblem. Martin Hoffman, 1737.</div></div></div>
<p>Linnaeus began his expedition from Uppsala on 12 May 1732, just before he turned 25.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-David_Black_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-David_Black-47">[45]</a></sup> He travelled on foot and horse, bringing with him his journal, botanical and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ornithological" title="Ornithological">ornithological</a> manuscripts and sheets of paper for pressing plants. Near <a href="/wiki/G%C3%A4vle" title="Gävle">Gävle</a> he found great quantities of <i>Campanula serpyllifolia</i>, later known as <i><a href="/wiki/Linnaea_borealis" title="Linnaea borealis">Linnaea borealis</a></i>, the twinflower that would become his favourite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt2001-42-43_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt2001-42-43-48">[46]</a></sup> He sometimes dismounted on the way to examine a flower or rock<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson43-44_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson43-44-49">[47]</a></sup> and was particularly interested in <a href="/wiki/Moss" title="Moss">mosses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lichen" title="Lichen">lichens</a>, the latter a main part of the diet of the <a href="/wiki/Reindeer" title="Reindeer">reindeer</a>, a common and economically important animal in Lapland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson46_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson46-50">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Linnaeus travelled clockwise around the coast of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Bothnia" title="Gulf of Bothnia">Gulf of Bothnia</a>, making major inland incursions from <a href="/wiki/Ume%C3%A5" title="Umeå">Umeå</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lule%C3%A5" title="Luleå">Luleå</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tornio" title="Tornio">Tornio</a>. He returned from his six-month-long, over 2,000 kilometres (1,200 mi) expedition in October, having gathered and observed many plants, birds and rocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt2001-63-65_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt2001-63-65-51">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt39-42_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt39-42-52">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Broberg29_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Broberg29-53">[51]</a></sup> Although Lapland was a region with limited <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>, Linnaeus described about 100 previously unidentified plants. These became the basis of his book <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Flora_Lapponica" title="Flora Lapponica">Flora Lapponica</a></i></span></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nationalgeographic1_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-nationalgeographic1-54">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stöver38-39_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stöver38-39-55">[53]</a></sup> However, on the expedition to Lapland, Linnaeus used Latin names to describe organisms because he had not yet developed the binomial system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-David_Black_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-David_Black-47">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Flora Lapponica</i></span></i> Linnaeus's ideas about <a href="/wiki/Nomenclature" title="Nomenclature">nomenclature</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_classification" title="Biological classification">classification</a> were first used in a practical way, making this the first proto-modern <a href="/wiki/Flora_(publication)" title="Flora (publication)">Flora</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frodin_p27_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frodin_p27-56">[54]</a></sup> The account covered 534 species, used the Linnaean classification system and included, for the described species, geographical distribution and taxonomic notes. It was <a href="/wiki/Augustin_Pyramus_de_Candolle" title="Augustin Pyramus de Candolle">Augustin Pyramus de Candolle</a> who attributed Linnaeus with <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Flora Lapponica</i></span></i> as the first example in the botanical genre of <a href="/wiki/Flora" title="Flora">Flora</a> writing. Botanical historian <a href="/wiki/Edward_Lee_Greene" title="Edward Lee Greene">E. L. Greene</a> described <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Flora Lapponica</i></span></i> as "the most classic and delightful" of Linnaeus's works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frodin_p27_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Frodin_p27-56">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>It was also during this expedition that Linnaeus had a <a href="/wiki/Eureka_effect" title="Eureka effect">flash of insight</a> regarding the classification of mammals. Upon observing the lower jawbone of a horse at the side of a road he was travelling, Linnaeus remarked: "If I only knew how many teeth and of what kind every animal had, how many teats and where they were placed, I should perhaps be able to work out a perfectly natural system for the arrangement of all quadrupeds."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt2001-54_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt2001-54-57">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1734, Linnaeus led a small group of students to <a href="/wiki/Dalarna" title="Dalarna">Dalarna</a>. Funded by the Governor of Dalarna, the expedition was to catalogue known natural resources and discover new ones, but also to gather intelligence on Norwegian mining activities at <a href="/wiki/R%C3%B8ros" title="Røros">Røros</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Broberg29_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Broberg29-53">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Years_in_the_Dutch_Republic_.281735.E2.80.9338.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Years_in_the_Dutch_Republic_(1735–38)">Years in the Dutch Republic (1735–38)</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Albertus_Seba_-_Hydra.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1724" data-file-width="2230" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Albertus_Seba_-_Hydra.jpg/220px-Albertus_Seba_-_Hydra.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Albertus_Seba_-_Hydra.jpg/330px-Albertus_Seba_-_Hydra.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Albertus_Seba_-_Hydra.jpg/440px-Albertus_Seba_-_Hydra.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Albertus_Seba_-_Hydra.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Hamburg Hydra, from the <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Thesaurus</i></span></i> (1734) of <a href="/wiki/Albertus_Seba" title="Albertus Seba">Albertus Seba</a>. Linnaeus identified the hydra specimen as a fake in 1735.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:190px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hartekamp-2011.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1502" data-file-width="2508" decoding="async" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Hartekamp-2011.jpg/188px-Hartekamp-2011.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Hartekamp-2011.jpg/282px-Hartekamp-2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Hartekamp-2011.jpg/376px-Hartekamp-2011.jpg 2x" width="188"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hartekamp-2011.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>View of <a href="/wiki/Hartekamp" title="Hartekamp">Hartekamp</a>, where Carl von Linné lived and studied for three years, from 1735 until 1738</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:190px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Musa_Cliffortiana_1736.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="624" decoding="async" height="362" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Musa_Cliffortiana_1736.jpg/188px-Musa_Cliffortiana_1736.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Musa_Cliffortiana_1736.jpg/282px-Musa_Cliffortiana_1736.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Musa_Cliffortiana_1736.jpg/376px-Musa_Cliffortiana_1736.jpg 2x" width="188"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Musa_Cliffortiana_1736.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Title page of <i><a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Musa_Cliffortiana" title="de:Musa Cliffortiana">Musa Cliffortiana</a></i> (1736), Linnaeus's first botanical monograph.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:190px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hortus_Cliffortianus_1737.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="651" decoding="async" height="347" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Hortus_Cliffortianus_1737.jpg/188px-Hortus_Cliffortianus_1737.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Hortus_Cliffortianus_1737.jpg/282px-Hortus_Cliffortianus_1737.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Hortus_Cliffortianus_1737.jpg/376px-Hortus_Cliffortianus_1737.jpg 2x" width="188"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hortus_Cliffortianus_1737.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Title page of <i><a href="/wiki/Hortus_Cliffortianus" title="Hortus Cliffortianus">Hortus Cliffortianus</a></i> (1737). The work was a collaboration between Linnaeus and <a href="/wiki/Georg_Dionysius_Ehret" title="Georg Dionysius Ehret">Georg Dionysius Ehret</a>, financed by <a href="/wiki/George_Clifford_III" title="George Clifford III">George Clifford III</a>, one of the directors of the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_India_Company" title="Dutch East India Company">VOC</a>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Doctorate">Doctorate</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Karte_Linne.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1471" data-file-width="1767" decoding="async" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Karte_Linne.png/220px-Karte_Linne.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Karte_Linne.png/330px-Karte_Linne.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Karte_Linne.png/440px-Karte_Linne.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Karte_Linne.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cities where he worked; those outside Sweden were only visited during 1735–1738.</div></div></div>
<p>His relations with Nils Rosén having worsened, Linnaeus accepted an invitation from Claes Sohlberg, son of a mining inspector, to spend the Christmas holiday in <a href="/wiki/Falun" title="Falun">Falun</a>, where Linnaeus was permitted to visit the mines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt2001-74_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt2001-74-58">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 1735, at the suggestion of Sohlberg's father, Linnaeus and Sohlberg set out for the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">Dutch Republic</a>, where Linnaeus intended to study medicine at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Harderwijk" title="University of Harderwijk">University of Harderwijk</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stöver71_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stöver71-59">[57]</a></sup> while tutoring Sohlberg in exchange for an annual salary. At the time, it was common for Swedes to pursue doctoral degrees in the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, then a highly revered place to study natural history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt2001-78-79_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt2001-78-79-60">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the way, the pair stopped in <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a>, where they met the mayor, who proudly showed them a supposed wonder of nature in his possession: the <a href="/wiki/Taxidermy" title="Taxidermy">taxidermied</a> remains of a seven-headed <a href="/wiki/Lernaean_Hydra" title="Lernaean Hydra">hydra</a>. Linnaeus quickly discovered the specimen was a <a href="/wiki/Hoax" title="Hoax">fake</a>, cobbled together from the jaws and paws of weasels and the skins of snakes. The provenance of the hydra suggested to Linnaeus that it had been manufactured by monks to represent the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Beast_(Bible)" title="The Beast (Bible)">Beast of Revelation</a>. Even at the risk of incurring the mayor's wrath, Linnaeus made his observations public, dashing the mayor's dreams of selling the hydra for an enormous sum. Linnaeus and Sohlberg were forced to flee from Hamburg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson60-61_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson60-61-61">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt90_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt90-62">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Linnaeus began working towards his degree as soon as he reached <a href="/wiki/Harderwijk" title="Harderwijk">Harderwijk</a>, a university known for awarding degrees in as little as a week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt94_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt94-63">[61]</a></sup> He submitted a dissertation, written back in Sweden, entitled <i>Dissertatio medica inauguralis in qua exhibetur hypothesis nova de febrium intermittentium causa</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dissertatio_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-dissertatio-64">[note 3]</a></sup> in which he laid out his hypothesis that <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a> arose only in areas with clay-rich soils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hempelmann_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-hempelmann-65">[62]</a></sup> Although he failed to identify the true source of disease transmission, (i.e., the <i><a href="/wiki/Anopheles" title="Anopheles">Anopheles</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Mosquito" title="Mosquito">mosquito</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[63]</a></sup> he did correctly predict that <i><a href="/wiki/Artemisia_annua" title="Artemisia annua">Artemisia annua</a></i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artemisinins" title="Artemisinins">wormwood</a>) would become a source of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antimalarial" title="Antimalarial">antimalarial</a> medications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hempelmann_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-hempelmann-65">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Within two weeks he had completed his oral and practical examinations and was awarded a doctoral degree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson60-61_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson60-61-61">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt94_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt94-63">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>That summer Linnaeus reunited with <a href="/wiki/Peter_Artedi" title="Peter Artedi">Peter Artedi</a>, a friend from Uppsala with whom he had once made a pact that should either of the two predecease the other, the survivor would finish the decedent's work. Ten weeks later, Artedi drowned in the <a href="/wiki/Canals_of_Amsterdam" title="Canals of Amsterdam">canals of Amsterdam</a>, leaving behind an unfinished manuscript on the classification of fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson66_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson66-67">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt98-100_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt98-100-68">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Publishing_of_Systema_Naturae">Publishing of <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Systema Naturae</i></span></i></span></h3>
<p>One of the first scientists Linnaeus met in the Netherlands was <a href="/wiki/Jan_Frederik_Gronovius" title="Jan Frederik Gronovius">Johan Frederik Gronovius</a>, to whom Linnaeus showed one of the several manuscripts he had brought with him from Sweden. The manuscript described a new system for classifying plants. When Gronovius saw it, he was very impressed, and offered to help pay for the printing. With an additional monetary contribution by the Scottish doctor <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Lawson" title="Isaac Lawson">Isaac Lawson</a>, the manuscript was published as <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Systema_Naturae" title="Systema Naturae">Systema Naturae</a></i></span></i> (1735).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt98_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt98-69">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson62-63_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson62-63-70">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>Linnaeus became acquainted with one of the most respected physicians and botanists in the Netherlands, <a href="/wiki/Herman_Boerhaave" title="Herman Boerhaave">Herman Boerhaave</a>, who tried to convince Linnaeus to make a career there. Boerhaave offered him a journey to South Africa and America, but Linnaeus declined, stating he would not stand the heat. Instead, Boerhaave convinced Linnaeus that he should visit the botanist <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Burman" title="Johannes Burman">Johannes Burman</a>. After his visit, Burman, impressed with his guest's knowledge, decided Linnaeus should stay with him during the winter. During his stay, Linnaeus helped Burman with his <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Thesaurus Zeylanicus</i></span></i>. Burman also helped Linnaeus with the books on which he was working: <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Fundamenta Botanica</i></span></i> and <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Bibliotheca Botanica</i></span></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt100-102_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt100-102-71">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="George_Clifford.2C_Philip_Miller.2C_and_Johann_Jacob_Dillenius"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="George_Clifford,_Philip_Miller,_and_Johann_Jacob_Dillenius">George Clifford, Philip Miller, and Johann Jacob Dillenius</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:221px;max-width:221px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:109px;max-width:109px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hortus_Cliffortianus_folia_simpl.png"><img alt="Folia Simplicia" data-file-height="589" data-file-width="357" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Hortus_Cliffortianus_folia_simpl.png/107px-Hortus_Cliffortianus_folia_simpl.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Hortus_Cliffortianus_folia_simpl.png/161px-Hortus_Cliffortianus_folia_simpl.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Hortus_Cliffortianus_folia_simpl.png/214px-Hortus_Cliffortianus_folia_simpl.png 2x" width="107"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:108px;max-width:108px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hortus_Cliffortianus_folia_compos.png"><img alt="Folia Composita et Folia Determinata" data-file-height="592" data-file-width="354" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Hortus_Cliffortianus_folia_compos.png/106px-Hortus_Cliffortianus_folia_compos.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Hortus_Cliffortianus_folia_compos.png/159px-Hortus_Cliffortianus_folia_compos.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Hortus_Cliffortianus_folia_compos.png/212px-Hortus_Cliffortianus_folia_compos.png 2x" width="106"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Leaf forms from <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Hortus_Cliffortianus" title="Hortus Cliffortianus">Hortus Cliffortianus</a></i></span></i></div></div></div></div>
<p>In August 1735, during Linnaeus's stay with Burman, he met <a href="/wiki/George_Clifford_III" title="George Clifford III">George Clifford III</a>, a director of the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_India_Company" title="Dutch East India Company">Dutch East India Company</a> and the owner of a rich botanical garden at the estate of <a href="/wiki/Hartekamp" title="Hartekamp">Hartekamp</a> in <a href="/wiki/Heemstede" title="Heemstede">Heemstede</a>. Clifford was very impressed with Linnaeus's ability to classify plants, and invited him to become his physician and superintendent of his garden. Linnaeus had already agreed to stay with Burman over the winter, and could thus not accept immediately. However, Clifford offered to compensate Burman by offering him a copy of <a href="/wiki/Hans_Sloane" title="Hans Sloane">Sir Hans Sloane's</a> <i>Natural History of Jamaica</i>, a rare book, if he let Linnaeus stay with him, and Burman accepted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson64_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson64-72">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stöver81-82_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stöver81-82-73">[70]</a></sup> On 24 September 1735, Linnaeus moved to Hartekamp to become personal physician to Clifford, and curator of Clifford's herbarium. He was paid 1,000 <a href="/wiki/Florin" title="Florin">florins</a> a year, with free board and lodging. Though the agreement was only for a winter of that year, Linnaeus practically stayed there until 1738.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[71]</a></sup> It was here that he wrote a book <i>Hortus Cliffortianus</i>, in the preface of which he described his experience as "the happiest time of my life". (A portion of Hartekamp was declared as public garden in April 1956 by the Heemstede local authority, and was named "Linnaeushof".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[72]</a></sup> It eventually became, as it is claimed, the biggest playground in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[73]</a></sup>)
</p><p>In July 1736, Linnaeus travelled to England, at Clifford's expense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt106-107_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt106-107-77">[74]</a></sup> He went to London to visit Sir Hans Sloane, a collector of natural history, and to see his <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_curiosities" title="Cabinet of curiosities">cabinet</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stöver89_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stöver89-78">[75]</a></sup> as well as to visit the <a href="/wiki/Chelsea_Physic_Garden" title="Chelsea Physic Garden">Chelsea Physic Garden</a> and its keeper, <a href="/wiki/Philip_Miller" title="Philip Miller">Philip Miller</a>. He taught Miller about his new system of subdividing plants, as described in <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Systema Naturae</i></span></i>. Miller was in fact reluctant to use the new <a href="/wiki/Binomial_nomenclature" title="Binomial nomenclature">binomial nomenclature</a>, preferring the classifications of <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Pitton_de_Tournefort" title="Joseph Pitton de Tournefort">Joseph Pitton de Tournefort</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Ray" title="John Ray">John Ray</a> at first. Linnaeus, nevertheless, applauded Miller's <i>Gardeners Dictionary</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[76]</a></sup> The conservative Scot actually retained in his dictionary a number of pre-Linnaean binomial signifiers discarded by Linnaeus but which have been retained by modern botanists. He only fully changed to the Linnaean system in the edition of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Gardeners_Dictionary" title="The Gardeners Dictionary">The Gardeners Dictionary</a></i> of 1768.  Miller ultimately was impressed, and from then on started to arrange the garden according to Linnaeus's system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stöver89-90_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stöver89-90-80">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>Linnaeus also travelled to Oxford University to visit the botanist <a href="/wiki/Johann_Jacob_Dillenius" title="Johann Jacob Dillenius">Johann Jacob Dillenius</a>. He failed to make Dillenius publicly fully accept his new classification system, though the two men remained in correspondence for many years afterwards. Linnaeus dedicated his <i>Critica Botanica</i> to him, as "<i>opus botanicum quo absolutius mundus non-vidit</i>". Linnaeus would later name a genus of tropical tree Dillenia in his honour. He then returned to Hartekamp, bringing with him many specimens of rare plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stöver90-93_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stöver90-93-81">[78]</a></sup> The next year, 1737, he published <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Genera_Plantarum" title="Genera Plantarum">Genera Plantarum</a></i></span></i>, in which he described 935 <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genera</a> of plants, and shortly thereafter he supplemented it with <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Corollarium Generum Plantarum</i></span></i>, with another sixty (<i>sexaginta</i>) genera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stöver95_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stöver95-82">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>His work at Hartekamp led to another book, <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Hortus_Cliffortianus" title="Hortus Cliffortianus">Hortus Cliffortianus</a></i></span></i>, a catalogue of the botanical holdings in the herbarium and botanical garden of Hartekamp. He wrote it in nine months (completed in July 1737), but it was not published until 1738.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt100-102_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt100-102-71">[68]</a></sup> It contains the first use of the name <i><a href="/wiki/Nepenthes" title="Nepenthes">Nepenthes</a></i>, which Linnaeus used to describe a genus of <a href="/wiki/Pitcher_plant" title="Pitcher plant">pitcher plants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nepenthes_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-nepenthes-84">[note 4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Linnaeus stayed with Clifford at Hartekamp until 18 October 1737 (new style), when he left the house to return to Sweden. Illness and the kindness of Dutch friends obliged him to stay some months longer in Holland. In May 1738, he set out for Sweden again. On the way home, he stayed in Paris for about a month, visiting botanists such as <a href="/wiki/Antoine_de_Jussieu" title="Antoine de Jussieu">Antoine de Jussieu</a>. After his return, Linnaeus never left Sweden again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt123_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt123-85">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koerner56_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koerner56-86">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Return_to_Sweden">Return to Sweden</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LinnaeusWeddingPortrait.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2536" data-file-width="2004" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/LinnaeusWeddingPortrait.jpg/170px-LinnaeusWeddingPortrait.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/LinnaeusWeddingPortrait.jpg/255px-LinnaeusWeddingPortrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/LinnaeusWeddingPortrait.jpg/340px-LinnaeusWeddingPortrait.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LinnaeusWeddingPortrait.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wedding portrait</div></div></div>
<p>When Linnaeus returned to Sweden on 28 June 1738, he went to <a href="/wiki/Falun" title="Falun">Falun</a>, where he entered into an engagement to <a href="/wiki/Sara_Elisabeth_Mor%C3%A6a" title="Sara Elisabeth Moræa">Sara Elisabeth Moræa</a>. Three months later, he moved to Stockholm to find employment as a physician, and thus to make it possible to support a family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nrm.se_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-nrm.se-87">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stöver141_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stöver141-88">[84]</a></sup> Once again, Linnaeus found a patron; he became acquainted with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carl_Gustav_Tessin" title="Carl Gustav Tessin">Count Carl Gustav Tessin</a>, who helped him get work as a physician at the Admiralty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stöver146-147_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stöver146-147-89">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koerner16_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koerner16-90">[86]</a></sup> During this time in Stockholm, Linnaeus helped found the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Swedish_Academy_of_Science" title="Royal Swedish Academy of Science">Royal Swedish Academy of Science</a>; he became the first <a href="/wiki/Praeses#Modern_uses" title="Praeses">Praeses</a> of the academy by drawing of lots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koerner103-105_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koerner103-105-91">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because his finances had improved and were now sufficient to support a family, he received permission to marry his fiancée, Sara Elisabeth Moræa. Their wedding was held 26 June 1739. Seventeen months later, Sara gave birth to their first son, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus_the_Younger" title="Carl Linnaeus the Younger">Carl</a>. Two years later, a daughter, <a href="/wiki/Elisabeth_Christina_von_Linn%C3%A9" title="Elisabeth Christina von Linné">Elisabeth Christina</a>, was born, and the subsequent year Sara gave birth to Sara Magdalena, who died when 15 days old. Sara and Linnaeus would later have four other children: Lovisa, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sara_Christina_von_Linn%C3%A9&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sara Christina von Linné (page does not exist)">Sara Christina</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://sv.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sara_Christina_von_Linn%C3%A9" title="sv:Sara Christina von Linné">sv</a>]</span>, Johannes and Sophia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nrm.se_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-nrm.se-87">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stöver382_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stöver382-92">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CarlvonLinne_house.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="735" data-file-width="918" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/CarlvonLinne_house.jpg/220px-CarlvonLinne_house.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/CarlvonLinne_house.jpg/330px-CarlvonLinne_house.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/CarlvonLinne_house.jpg/440px-CarlvonLinne_house.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CarlvonLinne_house.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>House in <a href="/wiki/Uppsala" title="Uppsala">Uppsala</a></div></div></div>
<p>In May 1741, Linnaeus was appointed Professor of Medicine at Uppsala University, first with responsibility for medicine-related matters. Soon, he changed place with the other Professor of Medicine, Nils Rosén, and thus was responsible for the Botanical Garden (which he would thoroughly reconstruct and expand), botany and <a href="/wiki/Natural_history" title="Natural history">natural history</a>, instead. In October that same year, his wife and nine-month-old son followed him to live in Uppsala.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gribbin_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gribbin-93">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 49–50">: 49–50 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id=".C3.96land_and_Gotland"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Öland_and_Gotland">Öland and Gotland</span></h3>
<p>Ten days after he was appointed Professor, he undertook an expedition to the island provinces of <a href="/wiki/%C3%96land" title="Öland">Öland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gotland" title="Gotland">Gotland</a> with six students from the university to look for plants useful in medicine. First, they travelled to Öland and stayed there until 21 June, when they sailed to <a href="/wiki/Visby" title="Visby">Visby</a> in Gotland. Linnaeus and the students stayed on Gotland for about a month, and then returned to Uppsala. During this expedition, they found 100 previously unrecorded plants. The observations from the expedition were later published in <i><span title="Swedish-language text"><i lang="sv">Öländska och Gothländska Resa</i></span></i>, written in Swedish. Like <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Flora Lapponica</i></span></i>, it contained both zoological and botanical observations, as well as observations concerning the culture in Öland and Gotland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koerner115_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koerner115-94">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt137-142_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt137-142-95">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the summer of 1745, Linnaeus published two more books: <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Flora Suecica</i></span></i> and <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Fauna Suecica</i></span></i>. <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Flora Suecica</i></span></i> was a strictly botanical book, while <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Fauna Suecica</i></span></i> was <a href="/wiki/Zoology" title="Zoology">zoological</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nrm.se_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-nrm.se-87">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stöver117-118_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stöver117-118-96">[92]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Anders_Celsius" title="Anders Celsius">Anders Celsius</a> had created the temperature scale <a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">named after him</a> in 1742. Celsius's scale was inverted compared to today, the boiling point at 0 °C and freezing point at 100 °C. In 1745, Linnaeus inverted the scale to its present standard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koerner204_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koerner204-97">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="V.C3.A4sterg.C3.B6tland"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Västergötland">Västergötland</span></h3>
<p>In the summer of 1746, Linnaeus was once again commissioned by the Government to carry out an expedition, this time to the Swedish province of <a href="/wiki/V%C3%A4sterg%C3%B6tland" title="Västergötland">Västergötland</a>. He set out from Uppsala on 12 June and returned on 11 August. On the expedition his primary companion was Erik Gustaf Lidbeck, a student who had accompanied him on his previous journey. Linnaeus described his findings from the expedition in the book <i><span title="Swedish-language text"><i lang="sv">Wästgöta-Resa</i></span></i>, published the next year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koerner115_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Koerner115-94">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt159_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt159-98">[94]</a></sup> After he returned from the journey, the Government decided Linnaeus should take on another expedition to the southernmost province <a href="/wiki/Scania" title="Scania">Scania</a>. This journey was postponed, as Linnaeus felt too busy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nrm.se_87-3"><a href="#cite_note-nrm.se-87">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1747, Linnaeus was given the title <a href="/wiki/Archiater" title="Archiater">archiater</a>, or chief physician, by the Swedish king <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Frederick_of_Sweden" title="Adolf Frederick of Sweden">Adolf Frederick</a>—a mark of great respect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt165_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt165-99">[95]</a></sup> The same year he was elected member of the <a href="/wiki/Prussian_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Prussian Academy of Sciences">Academy of Sciences in Berlin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stöver167_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stöver167-100">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scania">Scania</span></h3>
<p>In the spring of 1749, Linnaeus could finally journey to <a href="/wiki/Scania" title="Scania">Scania</a>, again commissioned by the Government. With him he brought his student, Olof Söderberg. On the way to Scania, he made his last visit to his brothers and sisters in Stenbrohult since his father had died the previous year. The expedition was similar to the previous journeys in most aspects, but this time he was also ordered to find the best place to grow <a href="/wiki/Walnut" title="Walnut">walnut</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sorbus_intermedia" title="Sorbus intermedia">Swedish whitebeam</a> trees; these trees were used by the military to make rifles. While there, they also visited the <a href="/wiki/Raml%C3%B6sa_H%C3%A4lsobrunn" title="Ramlösa Hälsobrunn">Ramlösa mineral spa</a>, where he remarked on the quality of its ferruginous water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[97]</a></sup> The journey was successful, and Linnaeus's observations were published the next year in <i><span title="Swedish-language text"><i lang="sv">Skånska Resa</i></span></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt198-205_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt198-205-102">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koerner116_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koerner116-103">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rector_of_Uppsala_University">Rector of Uppsala University</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:392px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CarlvonLinne_Hammarby.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="676" data-file-width="2798" decoding="async" height="94" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/CarlvonLinne_Hammarby.jpg/390px-CarlvonLinne_Hammarby.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/CarlvonLinne_Hammarby.jpg/585px-CarlvonLinne_Hammarby.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/CarlvonLinne_Hammarby.jpg/780px-CarlvonLinne_Hammarby.jpg 2x" width="390"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CarlvonLinne_Hammarby.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Summer home at his Hammarby estate</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CarlvonLinne_Garden.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/CarlvonLinne_Garden.jpg/220px-CarlvonLinne_Garden.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/CarlvonLinne_Garden.jpg/330px-CarlvonLinne_Garden.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/CarlvonLinne_Garden.jpg/440px-CarlvonLinne_Garden.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CarlvonLinne_Garden.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Linnaean_Garden" title="Linnaean Garden">Linnaean Garden</a> in Uppsala</div></div></div>
<p>In 1750, Linnaeus became rector of Uppsala University, starting a period where natural sciences were esteemed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nrm.se_87-4"><a href="#cite_note-nrm.se-87">[83]</a></sup> Perhaps the most important contribution he made during his time at Uppsala was to teach; many of his students travelled to various places in the world to collect botanical samples. Linnaeus called the best of these students his "apostles".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gribbin_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gribbin-93">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 56–57">: 56–57 </span></sup> His lectures were normally very popular and were often held in the Botanical Garden. He tried to teach the students to think for themselves and not trust anybody, not even him. Even more popular than the lectures were the botanical excursions made every Saturday during summer, where Linnaeus and his students explored the flora and fauna in the vicinity of Uppsala.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt206_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt206-104">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophia_Botanica"><i>Philosophia Botanica</i></span></h3>
<p>Linnaeus published <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophia_Botanica" title="Philosophia Botanica">Philosophia Botanica</a></i> in 1751.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELinnaeus1751_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinnaeus1751-105">[101]</a></sup> The book contained a complete survey of the taxonomy system he had been using in his earlier works. It also contained information of how to keep a journal on travels and how to maintain a botanical garden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt221_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt221-106">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrix_Noverca"><i>Nutrix Noverca</i></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nutrix_noverca.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1764" data-file-width="1272" decoding="async" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Nutrix_noverca.jpg/170px-Nutrix_noverca.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Nutrix_noverca.jpg/255px-Nutrix_noverca.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Nutrix_noverca.jpg/340px-Nutrix_noverca.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nutrix_noverca.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cover of <i>Nutrix Noverca</i> (1752)</div></div></div>
<p>During Linnaeus's time it was normal for upper class women to have <a href="/wiki/Wet_nurse" title="Wet nurse">wet nurses</a> for their babies. Linnaeus joined an ongoing campaign to end this practice in Sweden and promote breast-feeding by mothers. In 1752 Linnaeus published a thesis along with Frederick Lindberg, a physician student,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[103]</a></sup> based on their experiences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[104]</a></sup> In the tradition of the period, this dissertation was essentially an idea of the presiding reviewer (<i>prases</i>) expounded upon by the student. Linnaeus's dissertation was translated into French by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Emmanuel_Gilibert" title="Jean-Emmanuel Gilibert">J.E. Gilibert</a> in 1770 as <i>La Nourrice marâtre, ou Dissertation sur les suites funestes du nourrisage mercénaire</i>. Linnaeus suggested that children might absorb the personality of their wet nurse through the milk. He admired the child care practices of the Lapps<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koerner,_Lisbet_2009_69–70_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koerner,_Lisbet_2009_69–70-109">[105]</a></sup> and pointed out how healthy their babies were compared to those of Europeans who employed wet nurses. He compared the behaviour of wild animals and pointed out how none of them denied their newborns their breastmilk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koerner,_Lisbet_2009_69–70_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-Koerner,_Lisbet_2009_69–70-109">[105]</a></sup> It is thought that his activism played a role in his choice of the term <i>Mammalia</i> for the class of organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Species_Plantarum"><i>Species Plantarum</i></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Species_Plantarum" title="Species Plantarum">Species Plantarum</a></div>
<p>Linnaeus published <i><a href="/wiki/Species_Plantarum" title="Species Plantarum">Species Plantarum</a></i>, the work which is now internationally accepted as the starting point of modern <a href="/wiki/Botanical_nomenclature" title="Botanical nomenclature">botanical nomenclature</a>, in 1753.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stace_p24_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stace_p24-111">[107]</a></sup> The first volume was issued on 24 May, the second volume followed on 16 August of the same year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[note 5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[109]</a></sup> The book contained 1,200 pages and was published in two volumes; it described over 7,300 species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gribbin_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gribbin-93">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 47">: 47 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stöver198-199_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stöver198-199-115">[110]</a></sup> The same year the king dubbed him knight of the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Polar_Star" title="Order of the Polar Star">Order of the Polar Star</a>, the first civilian in Sweden to become a knight in this order. He was then seldom seen not wearing the order's insignia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt166_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt166-116">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ennoblement">Ennoblement</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Linne_CoA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="265" data-file-width="240" decoding="async" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Linne_CoA.jpg/220px-Linne_CoA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d6/Linne_CoA.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Linne_CoA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>His <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms" title="Coat of arms">coat of arms</a></div></div></div>
<p>Linnaeus felt Uppsala was too noisy and unhealthy, so he bought two farms in 1758: Hammarby and Sävja. The next year, he bought a neighbouring farm, Edeby. He spent the summers with his family at Hammarby; initially it only had a small one-storey house, but in 1762 a new, larger main building was added.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koerner116_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-Koerner116-103">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt219_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt219-117">[112]</a></sup> In Hammarby, Linnaeus made a garden where he could grow plants that could not be grown in the Botanical Garden in Uppsala. He began constructing a museum on a hill behind Hammarby in 1766, where he moved his library and collection of plants. A fire that destroyed about one third of Uppsala and had threatened his residence there necessitated the move.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt220-224_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt220-224-118">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the initial release of <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Systema Naturae</i></span></i> in 1735, the book had been expanded and reprinted several times; the <a href="/wiki/10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="10th edition of Systema Naturae">tenth edition</a> was released in 1758. This edition established itself as the starting point for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zoological_nomenclature" title="Zoological nomenclature">zoological nomenclature</a>, the equivalent of <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Species Plantarum</i></span></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gribbin_93-3"><a href="#cite_note-Gribbin-93">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 47">: 47 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt6_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt6-119">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Swedish King Adolf Frederick granted Linnaeus <a href="/wiki/Nobility" title="Nobility">nobility</a> in 1757, but he was not <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ennobled" title="Ennobled">ennobled</a> until 1761. With his ennoblement, he took the name Carl von Linné (Latinised as <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Carolus a Linné</i></span></i>), 'Linné' being a shortened and <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">gallicised</a> version of 'Linnæus', and the German <a href="/wiki/Nobiliary_particle" title="Nobiliary particle">nobiliary particle</a> '<a href="/wiki/Von" title="Von">von</a>' signifying his ennoblement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blunt171_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Blunt171-4">[3]</a></sup> The noble family's <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms" title="Coat of arms">coat of arms</a> prominently features a <a href="/wiki/Linnaea_borealis" title="Linnaea borealis">twinflower</a>, one of Linnaeus's favourite plants; it was given the scientific name <i>Linnaea borealis</i> in his honour by <a href="/wiki/Jan_Frederik_Gronovius" title="Jan Frederik Gronovius">Gronovius</a>. The shield in the coat of arms is divided into thirds: red, black and green for the three kingdoms of nature (animal, mineral and vegetable) in Linnaean classification; in the centre is an egg "to denote Nature, which is continued and perpetuated <i>in ovo</i>."
